=== BUGGY CODE ===

class Solution:
    def ways(self, pizza: List[str], k: int) -> int:
        self.r = len(pizza)
        self.c = len(pizza[0])
        
        # Step 1, pre-process the apple array and get the prefix sum
        tot_apples = 0
        self.pfsum_row = []
        self.pfsum_col = []
        
        for i in range(self.r):
            pfr = 0
            pfs_r = [0] * self.c
            pfs_c = [0] * self.c
            for j in range(self.c):
                if i > 0:
                    pfs_c[j] += self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]
                if pizza[i][j] == 'A':
                    pfr += 1
                    pfs_c[j] += 1
                    tot_apples += 1
                pfs_r[j] = pfr
            self.pfsum_row.append(pfs_r)
            self.pfsum_col.append(pfs_c)
        
        if tot_apples < k:
            return 0
        
        if k == 1:
            return 1
        
        return self.getWays(0, 0, k - 2) % (1000000007)
    
    
    @cache
    def getWays(self, i, j, k):
        if k == 1:
            # if only left one piece for cutting, we just need to check if there is any apple in the region
            found = False
            for c in range(j, self.c):
                apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][c]
                if i > 0:
                     apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][c]
                if apple_in_region:
                    found = True
                    break
            if found:
                return 1
            return 0
        else:
            # horizontally cut
            cannot_cut = True
            nr = i
            t_cnt = 0
            while nr < self.r - 1:
                # find the first row that we can start cutting
                while nr < self.r - 1 and cannot_cut:
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_row[nr][self.c - 1]
                    if j > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_row[nr][j - 1]
                    if apple_in_region:
                        cannot_cut = False
                    else:
                        nr += 1
                        
                if nr < self.r - 1:
                    t_cnt += self.getWays(nr + 1, j, k - 1)
                nr += 1
                
            # vertically cut
            cannot_cut = True
            nc = j
            while nc < self.c - 1:
                # find the first col that we can start cutting
                while nc < self. c - 1 and cannot_cut:
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][nc]
                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
                    if apple_in_region:
                        cannot_cut = False
                    else:
                        nc += 1
                
                if nc < self.c - 1:
                    t_cnt += self.getWays(i, nc + 1, k - 1)
                nc += 1
            
            return t_cnt

The problem asks us to fix the bugs in the provided Python code for the problem "Number of Ways of Cutting a Pizza" (LeetCode 1444).
The given code has a few logical bugs in the prefix sum calculation and the dynamic programming / recursion logic.

Let's carefully analyze the problem and the given code.
Given a rectangular pizza represented as a list of strings `pizza`, where `'A'` denotes an apple and `'.'` denotes an empty cell. We need to cut the pizza into `k` pieces using `k-1` cuts.
Each cut must be either vertical or horizontal, and we must give the top-left piece to a person, then continue cutting the remaining bottom-right rectangle.
Every piece given to a person must contain at least one apple.
We need to find the number of ways to cut the pizza such that every piece has at least one apple. Return the answer modulo 10^9 + 7.

Let's look at the given code's prefix sum logic.
`self.pfsum_row` and `self.pfsum_col` are computed.
Wait, the standard way to do this is a 2D prefix sum, but here the author tries to use row prefix sums and column prefix sums.
Let's trace `pfsum_row` and `pfsum_col`:
`pfs_r[j]` is the number of apples in row `i` from column 0 to `j`.
`pfs_c[j]` is the number of apples in column `j` from row 0 to `i`.
This is correct for getting the number of apples in a specific row range or column range.
However, in the `getWays` logic:
When checking if we can make a horizontal cut after row `nr`:
The top part is from row `i` to `nr`, and column `j` to `self.c - 1`.
The code checks `apple_in_region = self.pfsum_row[nr][self.c - 1] - self.pfsum_row[nr][j - 1]`.
This only checks if there's an apple in row `nr` from `j` to `c-1`. But the top part includes rows `i` to `nr`. Wait, no. If we cut after row `nr`, the top piece is rows `i` to `nr`. We just need to know if the top piece has at least one apple.
Actually, the condition to make a cut after row `nr` is that the top-left piece (rows `i` to `nr`, cols `j` to `c-1`) has at least one apple.
The code's logic for finding the first valid cut is:
```python
while nr < self.r - 1 and cannot_cut:
    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_row[nr][self.c - 1]
    if j > 0:
        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_row[nr][j - 1]
    if apple_in_region:
        cannot_cut = False
    else:
        nr += 1
```
Wait, this only checks row `nr`! It doesn't check if rows `i` to `nr-1` have apples. But if `cannot_cut` was true for `nr-1`, it means rows `i` to `nr-1` had no apples in columns `j` to `c-1`. So if row `nr` has an apple in columns `j` to `c-1`, then the region from `i` to `nr` has an apple. This part is actually correct for finding the *first* valid cut!

But what happens after the first valid cut?
After finding the first valid cut, the code does:
```python
if nr < self.r - 1:
    t_cnt += self.getWays(nr + 1, j, k - 1)
nr += 1
```
Wait, in the next iteration of the `while nr < self.r - 1` loop, `cannot_cut` is still `False`!
So it will add `self.getWays(nr + 1, j, k - 1)` for ALL subsequent rows `nr`, regardless of whether the top piece has an apple or not!
Ah! The condition `cannot_cut` is only used to find the *first* valid cut. For subsequent cuts, the top piece still needs to have at least one apple. But since `cannot_cut` is `False`, the inner `while` loop is skipped, and it just adds `getWays` for every subsequent `nr`.
This is a major bug. Every cut must leave at least one apple in the top piece.

Let's trace the horizontal cut logic again:

If we cut after `nr`, the top piece is `(i, j)` to `(nr, c-1)`.
The bottom piece is `(nr+1, j)` to `(r-1, c-1)`.
We need the top piece to have at least one apple.
Instead of the complex `cannot_cut` logic, we can just check if the top piece has at least one apple for each `nr`.
Actually, it's easier to use a 2D prefix sum to check if a rectangular region has any apples.
Let's rewrite the prefix sum logic to use a standard 2D prefix sum, or fix the current one.
If we use a 2D prefix sum, `apples[i][j]` is the number of apples in `(0,0)` to `(i-1, j-1)`.
Then the number of apples in `(r1, c1)` to `(r2, c2)` is:
`apples[r2+1][c2+1] - apples[r1][c2+1] - apples[r2+1][c1] + apples[r1][c1]`.

Let's see the current prefix sum logic.
`pfsum_row[i][j]` = apples in row `i` from col 0 to `j`.
`pfsum_col[i][j]` = apples in col `j` from row 0 to `i`.
This is enough to compute the 2D prefix sum, but it's easier to just build a 2D prefix sum.

Actually, LeetCode requires the answer modulo 10^9 + 7. It's best to do `t_cnt = (t_cnt + self.getWays(...)) % 1000000007`.

`nr = 1`: `apple_in_region` = 0. `cannot_cut` True. `nr = 2`.
`nr = 2`: `apple_in_region` = 1. `cannot_cut` False.
Adds `getWays(3, j, k-1)`.
This is correct.

What if `i = 1`, `j = 1`.
Row 1 has an apple in col 0, but no apple in col 1.
Row 2 has an apple in col 1.
`nr = 1`: `apple_in_region` for row 1 from col 1 to c-1 is 0. `cannot_cut` True. `nr = 2`.
`nr = 2`: `apple_in_region` for row 2 from col 1 to c-1 is 1. `cannot_cut` False.
Adds `getWays(3, j, k-1)`.
This is correct.

Wait! What if `i = 1`, `j = 1`.
Row 1 has an apple in col 1.
Row 2 has NO apple in col 1.
`nr = 1`: `apple_in_region` for row 1 from col 1 to c-1 is 1. `cannot_cut` False.
Adds `getWays(2, j, k-1)`.
`nr = 2`: inner loop skipped. Adds `getWays(3, j, k-1)`.
This is correct, because the top piece for `nr=2` is rows 1-2, cols 1-c-1, which has the apple from row 1.

Wait, is there ANY case where `cannot_cut` becomes False, but the top piece has NO apple?
No, because the check `apple_in_region > 0` guarantees that the current row `nr` has an apple in the required columns, which is part of the top piece.
So the top piece definitely has an apple.

Let's check `getWays` base case `k == 1`:
```python
        if k == 1:
            # if only left one piece for cutting, we just need to check if there is any apple in the region
            found = False
            for c in range(j, self.c):

                     apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][c]
```

Let's check the prefix sum calculation.
```python
        tot_apples = 0
        self.pfsum_row = []
        self.pfsum_col = []
        
        for i in range(self.r):
            pfr = 0
            pfs_r = [0] * self.c
            pfs_c = [0] * self.c
            for j in range(self.c):
                if i > 0:
                    pfs_c[j] += self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]
                if pizza[i][j] == 'A':
                    pfr += 1
                    pfs_c[j] += 1
                    tot_apples += 1
                pfs_r[j] = pfr
            self.pfsum_row.append(pfs_r)
            self.pfsum_col.append(pfs_c)
```
Wait, `pfs_c[j]` is initialized to 0. Then if `i > 0`, it adds `self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]`.
Then if there is an apple, it adds 1.
So `pfs_c[j]` will be the total apples in column `j` from row 0 to `i`.
This is correct.

Let's check `pfs_r[j]`.
`pfr` is initialized to 0.
If there is an apple, `pfr += 1`.
`pfs_r[j] = pfr`.
So `pfs_r[j]` is the total apples in row `i` from col 0 to `j`.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any bug in the prefix sum?
What if `j = 0`?
`pfs_r[0]` will be 1 if `pizza[i][0] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.
What if `i = 0`?
`pfs_c[j]` will not add `self.pfsum_col[-1][j]`. It will just be 1 if `pizza[0][j] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.

Let's check the usage of `pfsum_row` and `pfsum_col` in `getWays`.
```python
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_row[nr][self.c - 1]
                    if j > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_row[nr][j - 1]
```
This gets the number of apples in row `nr` from col `j` to `self.c - 1`. Correct.

```python
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][nc]
                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
```
This gets the number of apples in col `nc` from row `i` to `self.r - 1`. Correct.

```python
            # vertically cut
            cannot_cut = True

                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
                    if apple_in_region:
                        cannot_cut = False

                        nc += 1

```
This is perfectly symmetric with the horizontal cut logic and should be correct.

Let's review the bug `self.getWays(0, 0, k - 2)`.
Why did the author write `k - 2`?
Maybe they thought `k` is the number of cuts?
If `k` is the number of cuts, then `k - 1` cuts means `k` pieces.
But the base case is `if k == 1: return 1 if found else 0`.
If `k` was the number of cuts, the base case would be `if k == 0: return 1 if found else 0`.
But the comment says "if only left one piece for cutting", which means `k` is the number of pieces.
So `k - 2` is definitely a typo/mistake by the author. It should be `k`.

Wait, could it be that the author meant `k` is the number of cuts, and the base case was `k == 1` because they thought 1 cut means 2 pieces?
If `k` is the number of cuts, and we want `k` pieces, we pass `k - 1` cuts.
If we pass `k - 1` cuts, the base case should be `k == 0` cuts.
But the base case is `k == 1`.
So if the author meant `k` is the number of cuts, they would have written `k - 1` instead of `k - 2`?
Wait, if we want `k` pieces, we need `k - 1` cuts.
If we pass `k - 2`, and `k` is the number of cuts, then we are making `k - 2` cuts, which gives `k - 1` pieces. That doesn't match `k` pieces.
So `k` in `getWays` must be the number of pieces.
Then passing `k - 2` means we want `k - 2` pieces, which is wrong. It should be `k`.

`nc = 2`: col 2 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(0, 3, 2)`.

This seems completely correct.

Wait, is there any issue with `self.pfsum_row` and `self.pfsum_col` being lists of lists, and `@cache` on `getWays`?
`@cache` from `functools` requires the arguments to be hashable. `i, j, k` are integers, so they are hashable.
But `self` is also passed to `getWays`. Does `@cache` work with `self`?
Yes, `functools.cache` works with `self` in Python 3.9+.
Wait, `@cache` caches based on all arguments including `self`. Since `self` is the same instance, it will work, but it might hold a reference to `self` and prevent garbage collection, or it might just work fine. For LeetCode, it's standard to use `@cache` on methods.

Wait! If `cannot_cut` is True and `nr` reaches `self.r - 1`, the inner loop terminates.
Then it checks `if nr < self.r - 1`. This is False, so it doesn't add `getWays`.
Then `nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r`. The outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = self.r - 2`?
Inner loop terminates.
`nr < self.r - 1` is True. Adds `getWays(self.r - 1, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r - 1`.
Outer loop condition `nr < self.r - 1` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = 1`, and `self.r = 5`?
Inner loop terminates.
Adds `getWays(2, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 2`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped because `cannot_cut` is False.
Adds `getWays(3, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 3`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped.
Adds `getWays(4, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 4`.
Outer loop condition `nr < 4` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any possibility that `t_cnt` overflows?
Python handles big integers, but we should apply modulo.
Let's modify the code to apply modulo.
```python
        return self.getWays(0, 0, k) % (1000000007)
```
And inside `getWays`:
```python
            t_cnt += self.getWays(nr + 1, j, k - 1)
            t_cnt %= 1000000007
```
And similarly for vertical cuts.

Wait, is there any bug in the vertical cut logic?
```python
            # vertically cut
            cannot_cut = True
            nc = j
            while nc < self.c - 1:
                # find the first col that we can start cutting
                while nc < self. c - 1 and cannot_cut:
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][nc]
                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
                    if apple_in_region:
                        cannot_cut = False
                    else:
                        nc += 1
                
                if nc < self.c - 1:
                    t_cnt += self.getWays(i, nc + 1, k - 1)
                nc += 1
```
This is perfectly symmetric with the horizontal cut logic and should be correct.

Let's review the bug `self.getWays(0, 0, k - 2)`.
Why did the author write `k - 2`?
Maybe they thought `k` is the number of cuts?
If `k` is the number of cuts, then `k - 1` cuts means `k` pieces.
But the base case is `if k == 1: return 1 if found else 0`.
If `k` was the number of cuts, the base case would be `if k == 0: return 1 if found else 0`.
But the comment says "if only left one piece for cutting", which means `k` is the number of pieces.
So `k - 2` is definitely a typo/mistake by the author. It should be `k`.

Wait, could it be that the author meant `k` is the number of cuts, and the base case was `k == 1` because they thought 1 cut means 2 pieces?
If `k` is the number of cuts, and we want `k` pieces, we pass `k - 1` cuts.
If we pass `k - 1` cuts, the base case should be `k == 0` cuts.
But the base case is `k == 1`.
So if the author meant `k` is the number of cuts, they would have written `k - 1` instead of `k - 2`?
Wait, if we want `k` pieces, we need `k - 1` cuts.
If we pass `k - 2`, and `k` is the number of cuts, then we are making `k - 2` cuts, which gives `k - 1` pieces. That doesn't match `k` pieces.
So `k` in `getWays` must be the number of pieces.
Then passing `k - 2` means we want `k - 2` pieces, which is wrong. It should be `k`.

This seems completely correct.

`@cache` from `functools` requires the arguments to be hashable. `i, j, k` are integers, so they are hashable.
But `self` is also passed to `getWays`. Does `@cache` work with `self`?
Yes, `functools.cache` works with `self` in Python 3.9+.
Wait, `@cache` caches based on all arguments including `self`. Since `self` is the same instance, it will work, but it might hold a reference to `self` and prevent garbage collection, or it might just work fine. For LeetCode, it's standard to use `@cache` on methods.

Let's check the prefix sum calculation.
```python
        tot_apples = 0
        self.pfsum_row = []
        self.pfsum_col = []
        
        for i in range(self.r):
            pfr = 0
            pfs_r = [0] * self.c
            pfs_c = [0] * self.c
            for j in range(self.c):
                if i > 0:
                    pfs_c[j] += self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]
                if pizza[i][j] == 'A':
                    pfr += 1
                    pfs_c[j] += 1
                    tot_apples += 1
                pfs_r[j] = pfr
            self.pfsum_row.append(pfs_r)
            self.pfsum_col.append(pfs_c)
```
Wait, `pfs_c[j]` is initialized to 0. Then if `i > 0`, it adds `self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]`.
Then if there is an apple, it adds 1.
So `pfs_c[j]` will be the total apples in column `j` from row 0 to `i`.
This is correct.

Let's check `pfs_r[j]`.
`pfr` is initialized to 0.
If there is an apple, `pfr += 1`.
`pfs_r[j] = pfr`.
So `pfs_r[j]` is the total apples in row `i` from col 0 to `j`.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any bug in the prefix sum?

`pfs_r[0]` will be 1 if `pizza[i][0] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.

`pfs_c[j]` will not add `self.pfsum_col[-1][j]`. It will just be 1 if `pizza[0][j] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.

```python
            self.pfsum_col.append(pfs_c)
```
Wait, `pfs_c[j]` is initialized to 0. Then if `i > 0`, it adds `self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]`.
Then if there is an apple, it adds 1.
So `pfs_c[j]` will be the total apples in column `j` from row 0 to `i`.
This is correct.

Let's check `pfs_r[j]`.
`pfr` is initialized to 0.
If there is an apple, `pfr += 1`.
`pfs_r[j] = pfr`.
So `pfs_r[j]` is the total apples in row `i` from col 0 to `j`.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any bug in the prefix sum?
What if `j = 0`?
`pfs_r[0]` will be 1 if `pizza[i][0] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.
What if `i = 0`?
`pfs_c[j]` will not add `self.pfsum_col[-1][j]`. It will just be 1 if `pizza[0][j] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.

Let's check the usage of `pfsum_row` and `pfsum_col` in `getWays`.
```python
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_row[nr][self.c - 1]
                    if j > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_row[nr][j - 1]
```
This gets the number of apples in row `nr` from col `j` to `self.c - 1`. Correct.

```python
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][nc]
                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
```
This gets the number of apples in col `nc` from row `i` to `self.r - 1`. Correct.

Wait, are there any edge cases?
What if `k = 0`? The problem says `k >= 1`. So `k = 0` is not possible.
What if `pizza` is empty? The problem says `1 <= pizza.length, pizza[i].length <= 50`. So not empty.

Let's trace `k = 2`.
`self.getWays(0, 0, 2)`
`k = 2 != 1`, goes to `else`.
It will try horizontal and vertical cuts.
If it finds a valid cut, it calls `self.getWays(nr + 1, j, 1)`.
Then in `getWays(nr + 1, j, 1)`, `k == 1`, it checks if the remaining region has an apple. If so, returns 1.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any issue with `t_cnt` not being initialized in the original code?
```python
        else:
            # horizontally cut
            cannot_cut = True
            nr = i
            t_cnt = 0
```
`t_cnt` is initialized to 0. Correct.

Wait, let's look at the inner loop condition:
```python
                while nr < self.r - 1 and cannot_cut:
```
If `cannot_cut` is False, it breaks out of the inner loop.
Then it checks:
```python
                if nr < self.r - 1:
                    t_cnt += self.getWays(nr + 1, j, k - 1)
                nr += 1
```
Wait! If `cannot_cut` is True and `nr` reaches `self.r - 1`, the inner loop terminates.
Then it checks `if nr < self.r - 1`. This is False, so it doesn't add `getWays`.
Then `nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r`. The outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = self.r - 2`?
Inner loop terminates.
`nr < self.r - 1` is True. Adds `getWays(self.r - 1, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r - 1`.
Outer loop condition `nr < self.r - 1` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = 1`, and `self.r = 5`?
Inner loop terminates.
Adds `getWays(2, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 2`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped because `cannot_cut` is False.
Adds `getWays(3, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 3`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped.
Adds `getWays(4, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 4`.
Outer loop condition `nr < 4` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any possibility that `t_cnt` overflows?
Python handles big integers, but we should apply modulo.
Let's modify the code to apply modulo.
```python
        return self.getWays(0, 0, k) % (1000000007)
```
And inside `getWays`:
```python
            t_cnt += self.getWays(nr + 1, j, k - 1)
            t_cnt %= 1000000007
```
And similarly for vertical cuts.

Wait, is there any bug in the vertical cut logic?
```python
            # vertically cut
            cannot_cut = True
            nc = j
            while nc < self.c - 1:
                # find the first col that we can start cutting
                while nc < self. c - 1 and cannot_cut:
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][nc]
                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
                    if apple_in_region:
                        cannot_cut = False
                    else:
                        nc += 1
                
                if nc < self.c - 1:
                    t_cnt += self.getWays(i, nc + 1, k - 1)
                nc += 1
```
This is perfectly symmetric with the horizontal cut logic and should be correct.

Let's review the bug `self.getWays(0, 0, k - 2)`.
Why did the author write `k - 2`?
Maybe they thought `k` is the number of cuts?
If `k` is the number of cuts, then `k - 1` cuts means `k` pieces.
But the base case is `if k == 1: return 1 if found else 0`.
If `k` was the number of cuts, the base case would be `if k == 0: return 1 if found else 0`.
But the comment says "if only left one piece for cutting", which means `k` is the number of pieces.
So `k - 2` is definitely a typo/mistake by the author. It should be `k`.

Wait, could it be that the author meant `k` is the number of cuts, and the base case was `k == 1` because they thought 1 cut means 2 pieces?
If `k` is the number of cuts, and we want `k` pieces, we pass `k - 1` cuts.
If we pass `k - 1` cuts, the base case should be `k == 0` cuts.
But the base case is `k == 1`.
So if the author meant `k` is the number of cuts, they would have written `k - 1` instead of `k - 2`?
Wait, if we want `k` pieces, we need `k - 1` cuts.
If we pass `k - 2`, and `k` is the number of cuts, then we are making `k - 2` cuts, which gives `k - 1` pieces. That doesn't match `k` pieces.
So `k` in `getWays` must be the number of pieces.
Then passing `k - 2` means we want `k - 2` pieces, which is wrong. It should be `k`.

Let's double check if there's any other bug.
What if `pizza = ["A..", "AAA", "..."]`, `k = 3`?
`tot_apples = 4 >= 3`.
`getWays(0, 0, 3)`
`k = 3 != 1`.
Horizontal cuts:
`nr = 0`: row 0 has 'A'. `cannot_cut` = False. Adds `getWays(1, 0, 2)`.
`nr = 1`: row 1 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(2, 0, 2)`.
Vertical cuts:
`nc = 0`: col 0 has 'A'. `cannot_cut` = False. Adds `getWays(0, 1, 2)`.
`nc = 1`: col 1 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(0, 2, 2)`.
`nc = 2`: col 2 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(0, 3, 2)`.

This seems completely correct.

Wait, is there any issue with `self.pfsum_row` and `self.pfsum_col` being lists of lists, and `@cache` on `getWays`?
`@cache` from `functools` requires the arguments to be hashable. `i, j, k` are integers, so they are hashable.
But `self` is also passed to `getWays`. Does `@cache` work with `self`?
Yes, `functools.cache` works with `self` in Python 3.9+.
Wait, `@cache` caches based on all arguments including `self`. Since `self` is the same instance, it will work, but it might hold a reference to `self` and prevent garbage collection, or it might just work fine. For LeetCode, it's standard to use `@cache` on methods.

If `k` was the number of cuts, the base case would be `if k == 0: return 1 if found else 0`.
But the comment says "if only left one piece for cutting", which means `k` is the number of pieces.
So `k - 2` is definitely a typo/mistake by the author. It should be `k`.

Wait, could it be that the author meant `k` is the number of cuts, and the base case was `k == 1` because they thought 1 cut means 2 pieces?
If `k` is the number of cuts, and we want `k` pieces, we pass `k - 1` cuts.
If we pass `k - 1` cuts, the base case should be `k == 0` cuts.
But the base case is `k == 1`.
So if the author meant `k` is the number of cuts, they would have written `k - 1` instead of `k - 2`?
Wait, if we want `k` pieces, we need `k - 1` cuts.
If we pass `k - 2`, and `k` is the number of cuts, then we are making `k - 2` cuts, which gives `k - 1` pieces. That doesn't match `k` pieces.
So `k` in `getWays` must be the number of pieces.
Then passing `k - 2` means we want `k - 2` pieces, which is wrong. It should be `k`.

Let's double check if there's any other bug.
What if `pizza = ["A..", "AAA", "..."]`, `k = 3`?
`tot_apples = 4 >= 3`.
`getWays(0, 0, 3)`
`k = 3 != 1`.
Horizontal cuts:
`nr = 0`: row 0 has 'A'. `cannot_cut` = False. Adds `getWays(1, 0, 2)`.
`nr = 1`: row 1 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(2, 0, 2)`.
Vertical cuts:
`nc = 0`: col 0 has 'A'. `cannot_cut` = False. Adds `getWays(0, 1, 2)`.
`nc = 1`: col 1 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(0, 2, 2)`.
`nc = 2`: col 2 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(0, 3, 2)`.

This seems completely correct.

Wait, is there any issue with `self.pfsum_row` and `self.pfsum_col` being lists of lists, and `@cache` on `getWays`?
`@cache` from `functools` requires the arguments to be hashable. `i, j, k` are integers, so they are hashable.
But `self` is also passed to `getWays`. Does `@cache` work with `self`?
Yes, `functools.cache` works with `self` in Python 3.9+.
Wait, `@cache` caches based on all arguments including `self`. Since `self` is the same instance, it will work, but it might hold a reference to `self` and prevent garbage collection, or it might just work fine. For LeetCode, it's standard to use `@cache` on methods.

Let's consider if we should just use a 2D prefix sum. It's much simpler and less error-prone.

Bugs to fix:
1. `self.getWays(0, 0, k - 2)` -> `self.getWays(0, 0, k)`
2. Add modulo operation inside `getWays` to prevent large numbers and meet the problem requirements.
Wait, the original code does `return self.getWays(0, 0, k - 2) % (1000000007)`.
If I change it to `self.getWays(0, 0, k) % (1000000007)`, and add modulo inside `getWays`, it will be correct.

Let's check if there are any other bugs.
```python
        if tot_apples < k:
            return 0
        
        if k == 1:
            return 1
```
If `k == 1` and `tot_apples >= 1`, it returns 1. This is correct because we don't need to cut, and the whole pizza has at least 1 apple.
Wait, what if `tot_apples >= 1` and `k == 1`? It returns 1. Correct.

Let's check `getWays` base case `k == 1`:
```python
        if k == 1:
            # if only left one piece for cutting, we just need to check if there is any apple in the region
            found = False
            for c in range(j, self.c):
                apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][c]
                if i > 0:
                     apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][c]
                if apple_in_region:
                    found = True
                    break
            if found:
                return 1
            return 0
```
This is correct.

Let's check the prefix sum calculation.
```python
        tot_apples = 0
        self.pfsum_row = []
        self.pfsum_col = []
        
        for i in range(self.r):
            pfr = 0
            pfs_r = [0] * self.c
            pfs_c = [0] * self.c
            for j in range(self.c):
                if i > 0:
                    pfs_c[j] += self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]
                if pizza[i][j] == 'A':
                    pfr += 1
                    pfs_c[j] += 1
                    tot_apples += 1
                pfs_r[j] = pfr
            self.pfsum_row.append(pfs_r)
            self.pfsum_col.append(pfs_c)
```
Wait, `pfs_c[j]` is initialized to 0. Then if `i > 0`, it adds `self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]`.
Then if there is an apple, it adds 1.
So `pfs_c[j]` will be the total apples in column `j` from row 0 to `i`.
This is correct.

Let's check `pfs_r[j]`.
`pfr` is initialized to 0.
If there is an apple, `pfr += 1`.
`pfs_r[j] = pfr`.
So `pfs_r[j]` is the total apples in row `i` from col 0 to `j`.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any bug in the prefix sum?
What if `j = 0`?
`pfs_r[0]` will be 1 if `pizza[i][0] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.
What if `i = 0`?
`pfs_c[j]` will not add `self.pfsum_col[-1][j]`. It will just be 1 if `pizza[0][j] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.

Let's check the usage of `pfsum_row` and `pfsum_col` in `getWays`.
```python
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_row[nr][self.c - 1]
                    if j > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_row[nr][j - 1]
```
This gets the number of apples in row `nr` from col `j` to `self.c - 1`. Correct.

```python
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][nc]
                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
```
This gets the number of apples in col `nc` from row `i` to `self.r - 1`. Correct.

Wait, are there any edge cases?
What if `k = 0`? The problem says `k >= 1`. So `k = 0` is not possible.
What if `pizza` is empty? The problem says `1 <= pizza.length, pizza[i].length <= 50`. So not empty.

`self.getWays(0, 0, 2)`
`k = 2 != 1`, goes to `else`.

Then in `getWays(nr + 1, j, 1)`, `k == 1`, it checks if the remaining region has an apple. If so, returns 1.
This is correct.

```python
        else:
```

```python
                nr += 1
```
Wait! If `cannot_cut` is True and `nr` reaches `self.r - 1`, the inner loop terminates.
Then it checks `if nr < self.r - 1`. This is False, so it doesn't add `getWays`.
Then `nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r`. The outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = self.r - 2`?
Inner loop terminates.
`nr < self.r - 1` is True. Adds `getWays(self.r - 1, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r - 1`.
Outer loop condition `nr < self.r - 1` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = 1`, and `self.r = 5`?
Inner loop terminates.
Adds `getWays(2, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 2`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped because `cannot_cut` is False.
Adds `getWays(3, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 3`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped.
Adds `getWays(4, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 4`.
Outer loop condition `nr < 4` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any bug in the vertical cut logic?
```python
            # vertically cut
            cannot_cut = True
            nc = j
            while nc < self.c - 1:
                # find the first col that we can start cutting
                while nc < self. c - 1 and cannot_cut:
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][nc]
                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
                    if apple_in_region:
                        cannot_cut = False
                    else:
                        nc += 1
                
                if nc < self.c - 1:
                    t_cnt += self.getWays(i, nc + 1, k - 1)
                nc += 1
```
This is perfectly symmetric with the horizontal cut logic and should be correct.

What if `i = 0`?
`pfs_c[j]` will not add `self.pfsum_col[-1][j]`. It will just be 1 if `pizza[0][j] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.

Let's check the usage of `pfsum_row` and `pfsum_col` in `getWays`.
```python
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_row[nr][self.c - 1]
                    if j > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_row[nr][j - 1]
```
This gets the number of apples in row `nr` from col `j` to `self.c - 1`. Correct.

```python
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][nc]
                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
```
This gets the number of apples in col `nc` from row `i` to `self.r - 1`. Correct.

Wait, are there any edge cases?
What if `k = 0`? The problem says `k >= 1`. So `k = 0` is not possible.
What if `pizza` is empty? The problem says `1 <= pizza.length, pizza[i].length <= 50`. So not empty.

Let's trace `k = 2`.
`self.getWays(0, 0, 2)`
`k = 2 != 1`, goes to `else`.
It will try horizontal and vertical cuts.
If it finds a valid cut, it calls `self.getWays(nr + 1, j, 1)`.
Then in `getWays(nr + 1, j, 1)`, `k == 1`, it checks if the remaining region has an apple. If so, returns 1.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any issue with `t_cnt` not being initialized in the original code?
```python
        else:
            # horizontally cut
            cannot_cut = True
            nr = i
            t_cnt = 0
```
`t_cnt` is initialized to 0. Correct.

Wait, let's look at the inner loop condition:
```python
                while nr < self.r - 1 and cannot_cut:
```
If `cannot_cut` is False, it breaks out of the inner loop.
Then it checks:
```python
                if nr < self.r - 1:
                    t_cnt += self.getWays(nr + 1, j, k - 1)
                nr += 1
```
Wait! If `cannot_cut` is True and `nr` reaches `self.r - 1`, the inner loop terminates.
Then it checks `if nr < self.r - 1`. This is False, so it doesn't add `getWays`.
Then `nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r`. The outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = self.r - 2`?
Inner loop terminates.
`nr < self.r - 1` is True. Adds `getWays(self.r - 1, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r - 1`.
Outer loop condition `nr < self.r - 1` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = 1`, and `self.r = 5`?
Inner loop terminates.
Adds `getWays(2, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 2`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped because `cannot_cut` is False.
Adds `getWays(3, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 3`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped.
Adds `getWays(4, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 4`.
Outer loop condition `nr < 4` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any possibility that `t_cnt` overflows?
Python handles big integers, but we should apply modulo.
Let's modify the code to apply modulo.
```python
        return self.getWays(0, 0, k) % (1000000007)
```
And inside `getWays`:
```python
            t_cnt += self.getWays(nr + 1, j, k - 1)
            t_cnt %= 1000000007
```
And similarly for vertical cuts.

Wait, is there any bug in the vertical cut logic?
```python
            # vertically cut
            cannot_cut = True
            nc = j
            while nc < self.c - 1:
                # find the first col that we can start cutting
                while nc < self. c - 1 and cannot_cut:
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][nc]
                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
                    if apple_in_region:
                        cannot_cut = False
                    else:
                        nc += 1
                
                if nc < self.c - 1:
                    t_cnt += self.getWays(i, nc + 1, k - 1)
                nc += 1
```
This is perfectly symmetric with the horizontal cut logic and should be correct.

Let's review the bug `self.getWays(0, 0, k - 2)`.
Why did the author write `k - 2`?
Maybe they thought `k` is the number of cuts?
If `k` is the number of cuts, then `k - 1` cuts means `k` pieces.
But the base case is `if k == 1: return 1 if found else 0`.
If `k` was the number of cuts, the base case would be `if k == 0: return 1 if found else 0`.
But the comment says "if only left one piece for cutting", which means `k` is the number of pieces.
So `k - 2` is definitely a typo/mistake by the author. It should be `k`.

Wait, could it be that the author meant `k` is the number of cuts, and the base case was `k == 1` because they thought 1 cut means 2 pieces?
If `k` is the number of cuts, and we want `k` pieces, we pass `k - 1` cuts.
If we pass `k - 1` cuts, the base case should be `k == 0` cuts.
But the base case is `k == 1`.
So if the author meant `k` is the number of cuts, they would have written `k - 1` instead of `k - 2`?
Wait, if we want `k` pieces, we need `k - 1` cuts.
If we pass `k - 2`, and `k` is the number of cuts, then we are making `k - 2` cuts, which gives `k - 1` pieces. That doesn't match `k` pieces.
So `k` in `getWays` must be the number of pieces.
Then passing `k - 2` means we want `k - 2` pieces, which is wrong. It should be `k`.

Let's double check if there's any other bug.
What if `pizza = ["A..", "AAA", "..."]`, `k = 3`?
`tot_apples = 4 >= 3`.
`getWays(0, 0, 3)`
`k = 3 != 1`.
Horizontal cuts:
`nr = 0`: row 0 has 'A'. `cannot_cut` = False. Adds `getWays(1, 0, 2)`.
`nr = 1`: row 1 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(2, 0, 2)`.
Vertical cuts:
`nc = 0`: col 0 has 'A'. `cannot_cut` = False. Adds `getWays(0, 1, 2)`.
`nc = 1`: col 1 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(0, 2, 2)`.
`nc = 2`: col 2 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(0, 3, 2)`.

This seems completely correct.

Wait, is there any issue with `self.pfsum_row` and `self.pfsum_col` being lists of lists, and `@cache` on `getWays`?
`@cache` from `functools` requires the arguments to be hashable. `i, j, k` are integers, so they are hashable.
But `self` is also passed to `getWays`. Does `@cache` work with `self`?
Yes, `functools.cache` works with `self` in Python 3.9+.
Wait, `@cache` caches based on all arguments including `self`. Since `self` is the same instance, it will work, but it might hold a reference to `self` and prevent garbage collection, or it might just work fine. For LeetCode, it's standard to use `@cache` on methods.

Let's check `getWays` base case `k == 1`:
```python
        if k == 1:
            # if only left one piece for cutting, we just need to check if there is any apple in the region
            found = False
            for c in range(j, self.c):
                apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][c]
                if i > 0:
                     apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][c]
                if apple_in_region:
                    found = True
                    break
            if found:
                return 1
            return 0
```
This is correct.

Let's check the prefix sum calculation.
```python
        tot_apples = 0
        self.pfsum_row = []
        self.pfsum_col = []
        
        for i in range(self.r):
            pfr = 0
            pfs_r = [0] * self.c
            pfs_c = [0] * self.c
            for j in range(self.c):
                if i > 0:
                    pfs_c[j] += self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]
                if pizza[i][j] == 'A':
                    pfr += 1
                    pfs_c[j] += 1
                    tot_apples += 1
                pfs_r[j] = pfr
            self.pfsum_row.append(pfs_r)
            self.pfsum_col.append(pfs_c)
```
Wait, `pfs_c[j]` is initialized to 0. Then if `i > 0`, it adds `self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]`.
Then if there is an apple, it adds 1.
So `pfs_c[j]` will be the total apples in column `j` from row 0 to `i`.
This is correct.

Let's check `pfs_r[j]`.
`pfr` is initialized to 0.
If there is an apple, `pfr += 1`.
`pfs_r[j] = pfr`.
So `pfs_r[j]` is the total apples in row `i` from col 0 to `j`.
This is correct.

If we pass `k - 2`, and `k` is the number of cuts, then we are making `k - 2` cuts, which gives `k - 1` pieces. That doesn't match `k` pieces.
So `k` in `getWays` must be the number of pieces.
Then passing `k - 2` means we want `k - 2` pieces, which is wrong. It should be `k`.

Let's double check if there's any other bug.
What if `pizza = ["A..", "AAA", "..."]`, `k = 3`?
`tot_apples = 4 >= 3`.
`getWays(0, 0, 3)`
`k = 3 != 1`.
Horizontal cuts:
`nr = 0`: row 0 has 'A'. `cannot_cut` = False. Adds `getWays(1, 0, 2)`.
`nr = 1`: row 1 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(2, 0, 2)`.
Vertical cuts:
`nc = 0`: col 0 has 'A'. `cannot_cut` = False. Adds `getWays(0, 1, 2)`.
`nc = 1`: col 1 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(0, 2, 2)`.
`nc = 2`: col 2 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(0, 3, 2)`.

This seems completely correct.

Wait, is there any issue with `self.pfsum_row` and `self.pfsum_col` being lists of lists, and `@cache` on `getWays`?
`@cache` from `functools` requires the arguments to be hashable. `i, j, k` are integers, so they are hashable.
But `self` is also passed to `getWays`. Does `@cache` work with `self`?
Yes, `functools.cache` works with `self` in Python 3.9+.
Wait, `@cache` caches based on all arguments including `self`. Since `self` is the same instance, it will work, but it might hold a reference to `self` and prevent garbage collection, or it might just work fine. For LeetCode, it's standard to use `@cache` on methods.

Let's consider if we should just use a 2D prefix sum. It's much simpler and less error-prone.
But the prompt says "Fix the code and output only the fixed code." I should keep the original structure as much as possible and just fix the bugs.

Bugs to fix:
1. `self.getWays(0, 0, k - 2)` -> `self.getWays(0, 0, k)`
2. Add modulo operation inside `getWays` to prevent large numbers and meet the problem requirements.
Wait, the original code does `return self.getWays(0, 0, k - 2) % (1000000007)`.
If I change it to `self.getWays(0, 0, k) % (1000000007)`, and add modulo inside `getWays`, it will be correct.

Let's check if there are any other bugs.
```python
        if tot_apples < k:
            return 0
        
        if k == 1:
            return 1
```
If `k == 1` and `tot_apples >= 1`, it returns 1. This is correct because we don't need to cut, and the whole pizza has at least 1 apple.
Wait, what if `tot_apples >= 1` and `k == 1`? It returns 1. Correct.

Let's check `getWays` base case `k == 1`:
```python
        if k == 1:
            # if only left one piece for cutting, we just need to check if there is any apple in the region
            found = False
            for c in range(j, self.c):
                apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][c]
                if i > 0:
                     apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][c]
                if apple_in_region:
                    found = True
                    break
            if found:
                return 1
            return 0
```
This is correct.

Let's check the prefix sum calculation.
```python
        tot_apples = 0
        self.pfsum_row = []
        self.pfsum_col = []
        
        for i in range(self.r):
            pfr = 0
            pfs_r = [0] * self.c
            pfs_c = [0] * self.c
            for j in range(self.c):
                if i > 0:
                    pfs_c[j] += self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]
                if pizza[i][j] == 'A':
                    pfr += 1
                    pfs_c[j] += 1
                    tot_apples += 1
                pfs_r[j] = pfr
            self.pfsum_row.append(pfs_r)
            self.pfsum_col.append(pfs_c)
```
Wait, `pfs_c[j]` is initialized to 0. Then if `i > 0`, it adds `self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]`.
Then if there is an apple, it adds 1.
So `pfs_c[j]` will be the total apples in column `j` from row 0 to `i`.
This is correct.

Let's check `pfs_r[j]`.
`pfr` is initialized to 0.
If there is an apple, `pfr += 1`.
`pfs_r[j] = pfr`.
So `pfs_r[j]` is the total apples in row `i` from col 0 to `j`.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any bug in the prefix sum?
What if `j = 0`?
`pfs_r[0]` will be 1 if `pizza[i][0] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.
What if `i = 0`?
`pfs_c[j]` will not add `self.pfsum_col[-1][j]`. It will just be 1 if `pizza[0][j] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.

Let's check the usage of `pfsum_row` and `pfsum_col` in `getWays`.
```python
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_row[nr][self.c - 1]
                    if j > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_row[nr][j - 1]
```
This gets the number of apples in row `nr` from col `j` to `self.c - 1`. Correct.

```python
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][nc]
                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
```
This gets the number of apples in col `nc` from row `i` to `self.r - 1`. Correct.

Wait, are there any edge cases?
What if `k = 0`? The problem says `k >= 1`. So `k = 0` is not possible.
What if `pizza` is empty? The problem says `1 <= pizza.length, pizza[i].length <= 50`. So not empty.

Let's trace `k = 2`.
`self.getWays(0, 0, 2)`
`k = 2 != 1`, goes to `else`.
It will try horizontal and vertical cuts.
If it finds a valid cut, it calls `self.getWays(nr + 1, j, 1)`.
Then in `getWays(nr + 1, j, 1)`, `k == 1`, it checks if the remaining region has an apple. If so, returns 1.
This is correct.

```python
        else:

            nr = i

```
`t_cnt` is initialized to 0. Correct.


```python
                if nr < self.r - 1:

                nr += 1
```
Wait! If `cannot_cut` is True and `nr` reaches `self.r - 1`, the inner loop terminates.
Then it checks `if nr < self.r - 1`. This is False, so it doesn't add `getWays`.
Then `nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r`. The outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = self.r - 2`?
Inner loop terminates.
`nr < self.r - 1` is True. Adds `getWays(self.r - 1, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r - 1`.
Outer loop condition `nr < self.r - 1` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = 1`, and `self.r = 5`?
Inner loop terminates.
Adds `getWays(2, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 2`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped because `cannot_cut` is False.
Adds `getWays(3, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 3`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped.
Adds `getWays(4, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 4`.
Outer loop condition `nr < 4` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any possibility that `t_cnt` overflows?

Wait, is there any bug in the vertical cut logic?
```python
            # vertically cut
            cannot_cut = True
            nc = j
            while nc < self.c - 1:
                # find the first col that we can start cutting
                while nc < self. c - 1 and cannot_cut:
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][nc]
                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
                    if apple_in_region:
                        cannot_cut = False
                    else:
                        nc += 1
                
                if nc < self.c - 1:
                    t_cnt += self.getWays(i, nc + 1, k - 1)
                nc += 1
```
This is perfectly symmetric with the horizontal cut logic and should be correct.

```python
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
```
This gets the number of apples in col `nc` from row `i` to `self.r - 1`. Correct.

Wait, are there any edge cases?
What if `k = 0`? The problem says `k >= 1`. So `k = 0` is not possible.
What if `pizza` is empty? The problem says `1 <= pizza.length, pizza[i].length <= 50`. So not empty.

Let's trace `k = 2`.
`self.getWays(0, 0, 2)`
`k = 2 != 1`, goes to `else`.
It will try horizontal and vertical cuts.
If it finds a valid cut, it calls `self.getWays(nr + 1, j, 1)`.
Then in `getWays(nr + 1, j, 1)`, `k == 1`, it checks if the remaining region has an apple. If so, returns 1.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any issue with `t_cnt` not being initialized in the original code?
```python
        else:
            # horizontally cut
            cannot_cut = True
            nr = i
            t_cnt = 0
```
`t_cnt` is initialized to 0. Correct.

Wait, let's look at the inner loop condition:
```python
                while nr < self.r - 1 and cannot_cut:
```
If `cannot_cut` is False, it breaks out of the inner loop.
Then it checks:
```python
                if nr < self.r - 1:
                    t_cnt += self.getWays(nr + 1, j, k - 1)
                nr += 1
```
Wait! If `cannot_cut` is True and `nr` reaches `self.r - 1`, the inner loop terminates.
Then it checks `if nr < self.r - 1`. This is False, so it doesn't add `getWays`.
Then `nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r`. The outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = self.r - 2`?
Inner loop terminates.
`nr < self.r - 1` is True. Adds `getWays(self.r - 1, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r - 1`.
Outer loop condition `nr < self.r - 1` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = 1`, and `self.r = 5`?
Inner loop terminates.
Adds `getWays(2, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 2`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped because `cannot_cut` is False.
Adds `getWays(3, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 3`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped.
Adds `getWays(4, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 4`.
Outer loop condition `nr < 4` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any possibility that `t_cnt` overflows?
Python handles big integers, but we should apply modulo.
Let's modify the code to apply modulo.
```python
        return self.getWays(0, 0, k) % (1000000007)
```
And inside `getWays`:
```python
            t_cnt += self.getWays(nr + 1, j, k - 1)
            t_cnt %= 1000000007
```
And similarly for vertical cuts.

Wait, is there any bug in the vertical cut logic?
```python
            # vertically cut
            cannot_cut = True
            nc = j
            while nc < self.c - 1:
                # find the first col that we can start cutting
                while nc < self. c - 1 and cannot_cut:
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][nc]
                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
                    if apple_in_region:
                        cannot_cut = False
                    else:
                        nc += 1
                
                if nc < self.c - 1:
                    t_cnt += self.getWays(i, nc + 1, k - 1)
                nc += 1
```
This is perfectly symmetric with the horizontal cut logic and should be correct.

Let's review the bug `self.getWays(0, 0, k - 2)`.
Why did the author write `k - 2`?
Maybe they thought `k` is the number of cuts?
If `k` is the number of cuts, then `k - 1` cuts means `k` pieces.
But the base case is `if k == 1: return 1 if found else 0`.
If `k` was the number of cuts, the base case would be `if k == 0: return 1 if found else 0`.
But the comment says "if only left one piece for cutting", which means `k` is the number of pieces.
So `k - 2` is definitely a typo/mistake by the author. It should be `k`.

Wait, could it be that the author meant `k` is the number of cuts, and the base case was `k == 1` because they thought 1 cut means 2 pieces?
If `k` is the number of cuts, and we want `k` pieces, we pass `k - 1` cuts.
If we pass `k - 1` cuts, the base case should be `k == 0` cuts.
But the base case is `k == 1`.
So if the author meant `k` is the number of cuts, they would have written `k - 1` instead of `k - 2`?
Wait, if we want `k` pieces, we need `k - 1` cuts.
If we pass `k - 2`, and `k` is the number of cuts, then we are making `k - 2` cuts, which gives `k - 1` pieces. That doesn't match `k` pieces.
So `k` in `getWays` must be the number of pieces.
Then passing `k - 2` means we want `k - 2` pieces, which is wrong. It should be `k`.

Let's double check if there's any other bug.
What if `pizza = ["A..", "AAA", "..."]`, `k = 3`?
`tot_apples = 4 >= 3`.
`getWays(0, 0, 3)`
`k = 3 != 1`.
Horizontal cuts:
`nr = 0`: row 0 has 'A'. `cannot_cut` = False. Adds `getWays(1, 0, 2)`.
`nr = 1`: row 1 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(2, 0, 2)`.
Vertical cuts:
`nc = 0`: col 0 has 'A'. `cannot_cut` = False. Adds `getWays(0, 1, 2)`.
`nc = 1`: col 1 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(0, 2, 2)`.
`nc = 2`: col 2 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(0, 3, 2)`.

This seems completely correct.

Wait, is there any issue with `self.pfsum_row` and `self.pfsum_col` being lists of lists, and `@cache` on `getWays`?
`@cache` from `functools` requires the arguments to be hashable. `i, j, k` are integers, so they are hashable.
But `self` is also passed to `getWays`. Does `@cache` work with `self`?
Yes, `functools.cache` works with `self` in Python 3.9+.
Wait, `@cache` caches based on all arguments including `self`. Since `self` is the same instance, it will work, but it might hold a reference to `self` and prevent garbage collection, or it might just work fine. For LeetCode, it's standard to use `@cache` on methods.

Let's consider if we should just use a 2D prefix sum. It's much simpler and less error-prone.

```python
        if tot_apples < k:
```

Let's check `getWays` base case `k == 1`:
```python
        if k == 1:
            # if only left one piece for cutting, we just need to check if there is any apple in the region
            found = False
            for c in range(j, self.c):
                apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][c]
                if i > 0:
                     apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][c]
                if apple_in_region:
                    found = True
                    break
            if found:
                return 1
            return 0
```
This is correct.

Let's check the prefix sum calculation.
```python
        tot_apples = 0
        self.pfsum_row = []
        self.pfsum_col = []
        
        for i in range(self.r):
            pfr = 0
            pfs_r = [0] * self.c
            pfs_c = [0] * self.c
            for j in range(self.c):
                if i > 0:
                    pfs_c[j] += self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]
                if pizza[i][j] == 'A':
                    pfr += 1
                    pfs_c[j] += 1
                    tot_apples += 1
                pfs_r[j] = pfr
            self.pfsum_row.append(pfs_r)
            self.pfsum_col.append(pfs_c)
```
Wait, `pfs_c[j]` is initialized to 0. Then if `i > 0`, it adds `self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]`.
Then if there is an apple, it adds 1.
So `pfs_c[j]` will be the total apples in column `j` from row 0 to `i`.
This is correct.

Let's check `pfs_r[j]`.
`pfr` is initialized to 0.
If there is an apple, `pfr += 1`.
`pfs_r[j] = pfr`.
So `pfs_r[j]` is the total apples in row `i` from col 0 to `j`.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any bug in the prefix sum?
What if `j = 0`?
`pfs_r[0]` will be 1 if `pizza[i][0] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.
What if `i = 0`?
`pfs_c[j]` will not add `self.pfsum_col[-1][j]`. It will just be 1 if `pizza[0][j] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.

Vertical cuts:
`nc = 0`: col 0 has 'A'. `cannot_cut` = False. Adds `getWays(0, 1, 2)`.
`nc = 1`: col 1 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(0, 2, 2)`.
`nc = 2`: col 2 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(0, 3, 2)`.

This seems completely correct.

Wait, is there any issue with `self.pfsum_row` and `self.pfsum_col` being lists of lists, and `@cache` on `getWays`?
`@cache` from `functools` requires the arguments to be hashable. `i, j, k` are integers, so they are hashable.
But `self` is also passed to `getWays`. Does `@cache` work with `self`?
Yes, `functools.cache` works with `self` in Python 3.9+.
Wait, `@cache` caches based on all arguments including `self`. Since `self` is the same instance, it will work, but it might hold a reference to `self` and prevent garbage collection, or it might just work fine. For LeetCode, it's standard to use `@cache` on methods.

Let's consider if we should just use a 2D prefix sum. It's much simpler and less error-prone.
But the prompt says "Fix the code and output only the fixed code." I should keep the original structure as much as possible and just fix the bugs.

Bugs to fix:
1. `self.getWays(0, 0, k - 2)` -> `self.getWays(0, 0, k)`
2. Add modulo operation inside `getWays` to prevent large numbers and meet the problem requirements.
Wait, the original code does `return self.getWays(0, 0, k - 2) % (1000000007)`.
If I change it to `self.getWays(0, 0, k) % (1000000007)`, and add modulo inside `getWays`, it will be correct.

Let's check if there are any other bugs.
```python
        if tot_apples < k:
            return 0
        
        if k == 1:
            return 1
```
If `k == 1` and `tot_apples >= 1`, it returns 1. This is correct because we don't need to cut, and the whole pizza has at least 1 apple.
Wait, what if `tot_apples >= 1` and `k == 1`? It returns 1. Correct.

Let's check `getWays` base case `k == 1`:
```python
        if k == 1:
            # if only left one piece for cutting, we just need to check if there is any apple in the region
            found = False
            for c in range(j, self.c):
                apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][c]
                if i > 0:
                     apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][c]
                if apple_in_region:
                    found = True
                    break
            if found:
                return 1
            return 0
```
This is correct.

Let's check the prefix sum calculation.
```python
        tot_apples = 0
        self.pfsum_row = []
        self.pfsum_col = []
        
        for i in range(self.r):
            pfr = 0
            pfs_r = [0] * self.c
            pfs_c = [0] * self.c
            for j in range(self.c):
                if i > 0:
                    pfs_c[j] += self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]
                if pizza[i][j] == 'A':
                    pfr += 1
                    pfs_c[j] += 1
                    tot_apples += 1
                pfs_r[j] = pfr
            self.pfsum_row.append(pfs_r)
            self.pfsum_col.append(pfs_c)
```
Wait, `pfs_c[j]` is initialized to 0. Then if `i > 0`, it adds `self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]`.
Then if there is an apple, it adds 1.
So `pfs_c[j]` will be the total apples in column `j` from row 0 to `i`.
This is correct.

Let's check `pfs_r[j]`.
`pfr` is initialized to 0.
If there is an apple, `pfr += 1`.
`pfs_r[j] = pfr`.
So `pfs_r[j]` is the total apples in row `i` from col 0 to `j`.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any bug in the prefix sum?
What if `j = 0`?
`pfs_r[0]` will be 1 if `pizza[i][0] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.
What if `i = 0`?
`pfs_c[j]` will not add `self.pfsum_col[-1][j]`. It will just be 1 if `pizza[0][j] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.

Let's check the usage of `pfsum_row` and `pfsum_col` in `getWays`.
```python
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_row[nr][self.c - 1]
                    if j > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_row[nr][j - 1]
```
This gets the number of apples in row `nr` from col `j` to `self.c - 1`. Correct.

```python
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][nc]
                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
```
This gets the number of apples in col `nc` from row `i` to `self.r - 1`. Correct.

Wait, are there any edge cases?
What if `k = 0`? The problem says `k >= 1`. So `k = 0` is not possible.
What if `pizza` is empty? The problem says `1 <= pizza.length, pizza[i].length <= 50`. So not empty.

Let's trace `k = 2`.
`self.getWays(0, 0, 2)`
`k = 2 != 1`, goes to `else`.
It will try horizontal and vertical cuts.
If it finds a valid cut, it calls `self.getWays(nr + 1, j, 1)`.
Then in `getWays(nr + 1, j, 1)`, `k == 1`, it checks if the remaining region has an apple. If so, returns 1.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any issue with `t_cnt` not being initialized in the original code?
```python
        else:
            # horizontally cut
            cannot_cut = True
            nr = i
            t_cnt = 0
```
`t_cnt` is initialized to 0. Correct.

Wait, let's look at the inner loop condition:
```python
                while nr < self.r - 1 and cannot_cut:
```
If `cannot_cut` is False, it breaks out of the inner loop.
Then it checks:
```python
                if nr < self.r - 1:
                    t_cnt += self.getWays(nr + 1, j, k - 1)
                nr += 1
```
Wait! If `cannot_cut` is True and `nr` reaches `self.r - 1`, the inner loop terminates.
Then it checks `if nr < self.r - 1`. This is False, so it doesn't add `getWays`.
Then `nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r`. The outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = self.r - 2`?
Inner loop terminates.
`nr < self.r - 1` is True. Adds `getWays(self.r - 1, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r - 1`.
Outer loop condition `nr < self.r - 1` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = 1`, and `self.r = 5`?
Inner loop terminates.
Adds `getWays(2, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 2`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped because `cannot_cut` is False.
Adds `getWays(3, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 3`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped.
Adds `getWays(4, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 4`.
Outer loop condition `nr < 4` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any possibility that `t_cnt` overflows?
Python handles big integers, but we should apply modulo.

```python
        return self.getWays(0, 0, k) % (1000000007)
```

Wait, is there any bug in the vertical cut logic?
```python
            # vertically cut
            cannot_cut = True
            nc = j
            while nc < self.c - 1:
                # find the first col that we can start cutting
                while nc < self. c - 1 and cannot_cut:
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][nc]
                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
                    if apple_in_region:
                        cannot_cut = False
                    else:
                        nc += 1
                
                if nc < self.c - 1:
                    t_cnt += self.getWays(i, nc + 1, k - 1)
                nc += 1
```
This is perfectly symmetric with the horizontal cut logic and should be correct.

Let's review the bug `self.getWays(0, 0, k - 2)`.
Why did the author write `k - 2`?
Maybe they thought `k` is the number of cuts?
If `k` is the number of cuts, then `k - 1` cuts means `k` pieces.
But the base case is `if k == 1: return 1 if found else 0`.
If `k` was the number of cuts, the base case would be `if k == 0: return 1 if found else 0`.
But the comment says "if only left one piece for cutting", which means `k` is the number of pieces.
So `k - 2` is definitely a typo/mistake by the author. It should be `k`.

Wait, could it be that the author meant `k` is the number of cuts, and the base case was `k == 1` because they thought 1 cut means 2 pieces?
If `k` is the number of cuts, and we want `k` pieces, we pass `k - 1` cuts.
If we pass `k - 1` cuts, the base case should be `k == 0` cuts.
But the base case is `k == 1`.
So if the author meant `k` is the number of cuts, they would have written `k - 1` instead of `k - 2`?
Wait, if we want `k` pieces, we need `k - 1` cuts.
If we pass `k - 2`, and `k` is the number of cuts, then we are making `k - 2` cuts, which gives `k - 1` pieces. That doesn't match `k` pieces.
So `k` in `getWays` must be the number of pieces.
Then passing `k - 2` means we want `k - 2` pieces, which is wrong. It should be `k`.

Wait, is there any issue with `t_cnt` not being initialized in the original code?
```python
        else:
            # horizontally cut
            cannot_cut = True
            nr = i
            t_cnt = 0
```
`t_cnt` is initialized to 0. Correct.

Wait, let's look at the inner loop condition:
```python
                while nr < self.r - 1 and cannot_cut:
```
If `cannot_cut` is False, it breaks out of the inner loop.
Then it checks:
```python
                if nr < self.r - 1:
                    t_cnt += self.getWays(nr + 1, j, k - 1)
                nr += 1
```
Wait! If `cannot_cut` is True and `nr` reaches `self.r - 1`, the inner loop terminates.
Then it checks `if nr < self.r - 1`. This is False, so it doesn't add `getWays`.
Then `nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r`. The outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = self.r - 2`?
Inner loop terminates.
`nr < self.r - 1` is True. Adds `getWays(self.r - 1, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r - 1`.
Outer loop condition `nr < self.r - 1` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = 1`, and `self.r = 5`?
Inner loop terminates.
Adds `getWays(2, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 2`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped because `cannot_cut` is False.
Adds `getWays(3, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 3`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped.
Adds `getWays(4, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 4`.
Outer loop condition `nr < 4` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any possibility that `t_cnt` overflows?
Python handles big integers, but we should apply modulo.
Let's modify the code to apply modulo.
```python
        return self.getWays(0, 0, k) % (1000000007)
```
And inside `getWays`:
```python
            t_cnt += self.getWays(nr + 1, j, k - 1)
            t_cnt %= 1000000007
```
And similarly for vertical cuts.

Wait, is there any bug in the vertical cut logic?
```python
            # vertically cut
            cannot_cut = True
            nc = j
            while nc < self.c - 1:
                # find the first col that we can start cutting
                while nc < self. c - 1 and cannot_cut:
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][nc]
                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
                    if apple_in_region:
                        cannot_cut = False
                    else:
                        nc += 1
                
                if nc < self.c - 1:
                    t_cnt += self.getWays(i, nc + 1, k - 1)
                nc += 1
```
This is perfectly symmetric with the horizontal cut logic and should be correct.

Let's review the bug `self.getWays(0, 0, k - 2)`.
Why did the author write `k - 2`?
Maybe they thought `k` is the number of cuts?
If `k` is the number of cuts, then `k - 1` cuts means `k` pieces.
But the base case is `if k == 1: return 1 if found else 0`.
If `k` was the number of cuts, the base case would be `if k == 0: return 1 if found else 0`.
But the comment says "if only left one piece for cutting", which means `k` is the number of pieces.
So `k - 2` is definitely a typo/mistake by the author. It should be `k`.

Wait, could it be that the author meant `k` is the number of cuts, and the base case was `k == 1` because they thought 1 cut means 2 pieces?
If `k` is the number of cuts, and we want `k` pieces, we pass `k - 1` cuts.
If we pass `k - 1` cuts, the base case should be `k == 0` cuts.
But the base case is `k == 1`.
So if the author meant `k` is the number of cuts, they would have written `k - 1` instead of `k - 2`?
Wait, if we want `k` pieces, we need `k - 1` cuts.
If we pass `k - 2`, and `k` is the number of cuts, then we are making `k - 2` cuts, which gives `k - 1` pieces. That doesn't match `k` pieces.
So `k` in `getWays` must be the number of pieces.
Then passing `k - 2` means we want `k - 2` pieces, which is wrong. It should be `k`.

Let's double check if there's any other bug.
What if `pizza = ["A..", "AAA", "..."]`, `k = 3`?
`tot_apples = 4 >= 3`.
`getWays(0, 0, 3)`
`k = 3 != 1`.
Horizontal cuts:
`nr = 0`: row 0 has 'A'. `cannot_cut` = False. Adds `getWays(1, 0, 2)`.
`nr = 1`: row 1 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(2, 0, 2)`.
Vertical cuts:
`nc = 0`: col 0 has 'A'. `cannot_cut` = False. Adds `getWays(0, 1, 2)`.
`nc = 1`: col 1 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(0, 2, 2)`.
`nc = 2`: col 2 has 'A'. Adds `getWays(0, 3, 2)`.

This seems completely correct.

Wait, is there any issue with `self.pfsum_row` and `self.pfsum_col` being lists of lists, and `@cache` on `getWays`?
`@cache` from `functools` requires the arguments to be hashable. `i, j, k` are integers, so they are hashable.
But `self` is also passed to `getWays`. Does `@cache` work with `self`?
Yes, `functools.cache` works with `self` in Python 3.9+.
Wait, `@cache` caches based on all arguments including `self`. Since `self` is the same instance, it will work, but it might hold a reference to `self` and prevent garbage collection, or it might just work fine. For LeetCode, it's standard to use `@cache` on methods.

Let's consider if we should just use a 2D prefix sum. It's much simpler and less error-prone.

```python
        if tot_apples < k:
```

Let's check `getWays` base case `k == 1`:
```python
        if k == 1:
            # if only left one piece for cutting, we just need to check if there is any apple in the region
            found = False
            for c in range(j, self.c):
                apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][c]
                if i > 0:
                     apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][c]
                if apple_in_region:
                    found = True
                    break
            if found:
                return 1
            return 0
```
This is correct.

Let's check the prefix sum calculation.
```python
        tot_apples = 0
        self.pfsum_row = []
        self.pfsum_col = []
        
        for i in range(self.r):
            pfr = 0
            pfs_r = [0] * self.c
            pfs_c = [0] * self.c
            for j in range(self.c):
                if i > 0:
                    pfs_c[j] += self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]
                if pizza[i][j] == 'A':
                    pfr += 1
                    pfs_c[j] += 1
                    tot_apples += 1
                pfs_r[j] = pfr
            self.pfsum_row.append(pfs_r)
            self.pfsum_col.append(pfs_c)
```
Wait, `pfs_c[j]` is initialized to 0. Then if `i > 0`, it adds `self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]`.
Then if there is an apple, it adds 1.
So `pfs_c[j]` will be the total apples in column `j` from row 0 to `i`.
This is correct.

Let's check `pfs_r[j]`.
`pfr` is initialized to 0.
If there is an apple, `pfr += 1`.
`pfs_r[j] = pfr`.
So `pfs_r[j]` is the total apples in row `i` from col 0 to `j`.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any bug in the prefix sum?
What if `j = 0`?
`pfs_r[0]` will be 1 if `pizza[i][0] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.
What if `i = 0`?
`pfs_c[j]` will not add `self.pfsum_col[-1][j]`. It will just be 1 if `pizza[0][j] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.

Wait, `@cache` caches based on all arguments including `self`. Since `self` is the same instance, it will work, but it might hold a reference to `self` and prevent garbage collection, or it might just work fine. For LeetCode, it's standard to use `@cache` on methods.

Let's consider if we should just use a 2D prefix sum. It's much simpler and less error-prone.
But the prompt says "Fix the code and output only the fixed code." I should keep the original structure as much as possible and just fix the bugs.

Bugs to fix:
1. `self.getWays(0, 0, k - 2)` -> `self.getWays(0, 0, k)`
2. Add modulo operation inside `getWays` to prevent large numbers and meet the problem requirements.
Wait, the original code does `return self.getWays(0, 0, k - 2) % (1000000007)`.
If I change it to `self.getWays(0, 0, k) % (1000000007)`, and add modulo inside `getWays`, it will be correct.

Let's check if there are any other bugs.
```python
        if tot_apples < k:
            return 0
        
        if k == 1:
            return 1
```
If `k == 1` and `tot_apples >= 1`, it returns 1. This is correct because we don't need to cut, and the whole pizza has at least 1 apple.
Wait, what if `tot_apples >= 1` and `k == 1`? It returns 1. Correct.

Let's check `getWays` base case `k == 1`:
```python
        if k == 1:
            # if only left one piece for cutting, we just need to check if there is any apple in the region
            found = False
            for c in range(j, self.c):
                apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][c]
                if i > 0:
                     apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][c]
                if apple_in_region:
                    found = True
                    break
            if found:
                return 1
            return 0
```
This is correct.

Let's check the prefix sum calculation.
```python
        tot_apples = 0
        self.pfsum_row = []
        self.pfsum_col = []
        
        for i in range(self.r):
            pfr = 0
            pfs_r = [0] * self.c
            pfs_c = [0] * self.c
            for j in range(self.c):
                if i > 0:
                    pfs_c[j] += self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]
                if pizza[i][j] == 'A':
                    pfr += 1
                    pfs_c[j] += 1
                    tot_apples += 1
                pfs_r[j] = pfr
            self.pfsum_row.append(pfs_r)
            self.pfsum_col.append(pfs_c)
```
Wait, `pfs_c[j]` is initialized to 0. Then if `i > 0`, it adds `self.pfsum_col[i - 1][j]`.
Then if there is an apple, it adds 1.
So `pfs_c[j]` will be the total apples in column `j` from row 0 to `i`.
This is correct.

Let's check `pfs_r[j]`.
`pfr` is initialized to 0.
If there is an apple, `pfr += 1`.
`pfs_r[j] = pfr`.
So `pfs_r[j]` is the total apples in row `i` from col 0 to `j`.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any bug in the prefix sum?
What if `j = 0`?
`pfs_r[0]` will be 1 if `pizza[i][0] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.
What if `i = 0`?
`pfs_c[j]` will not add `self.pfsum_col[-1][j]`. It will just be 1 if `pizza[0][j] == 'A'`, else 0. Correct.

Let's check the usage of `pfsum_row` and `pfsum_col` in `getWays`.
```python
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_row[nr][self.c - 1]
                    if j > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_row[nr][j - 1]
```
This gets the number of apples in row `nr` from col `j` to `self.c - 1`. Correct.

```python
                    apple_in_region = self.pfsum_col[self.r - 1][nc]
                    if i > 0:
                        apple_in_region -= self.pfsum_col[i - 1][nc]
```
This gets the number of apples in col `nc` from row `i` to `self.r - 1`. Correct.

Wait, are there any edge cases?
What if `k = 0`? The problem says `k >= 1`. So `k = 0` is not possible.
What if `pizza` is empty? The problem says `1 <= pizza.length, pizza[i].length <= 50`. So not empty.

Let's trace `k = 2`.
`self.getWays(0, 0, 2)`
`k = 2 != 1`, goes to `else`.
It will try horizontal and vertical cuts.
If it finds a valid cut, it calls `self.getWays(nr + 1, j, 1)`.
Then in `getWays(nr + 1, j, 1)`, `k == 1`, it checks if the remaining region has an apple. If so, returns 1.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any issue with `t_cnt` not being initialized in the original code?
```python
        else:
            # horizontally cut
            cannot_cut = True
            nr = i
            t_cnt = 0
```
`t_cnt` is initialized to 0. Correct.

Wait, let's look at the inner loop condition:
```python
                while nr < self.r - 1 and cannot_cut:
```
If `cannot_cut` is False, it breaks out of the inner loop.
Then it checks:
```python
                if nr < self.r - 1:
                    t_cnt += self.getWays(nr + 1, j, k - 1)
                nr += 1
```
Wait! If `cannot_cut` is True and `nr` reaches `self.r - 1`, the inner loop terminates.
Then it checks `if nr < self.r - 1`. This is False, so it doesn't add `getWays`.
Then `nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r`. The outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = self.r - 2`?
Inner loop terminates.
`nr < self.r - 1` is True. Adds `getWays(self.r - 1, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr == self.r - 1`.
Outer loop condition `nr < self.r - 1` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

What if `cannot_cut` becomes False at `nr = 1`, and `self.r = 5`?
Inner loop terminates.
Adds `getWays(2, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 2`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped because `cannot_cut` is False.
Adds `getWays(3, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 3`.
Outer loop continues.
Inner loop skipped.
Adds `getWays(4, j, k - 1)`.
`nr += 1`, so `nr = 4`.
Outer loop condition `nr < 4` is False. Outer loop terminates.
This is correct.

Wait, is there any possibility that `t_cnt` overflows?
Python handles big integers, but we should apply modulo.
Let's modify the code to apply modulo.
```python
        return self.getWays(0, 0, k) % (1000000007)
```
And inside `getWays`:
```python
            t_cnt += self.getWays(nr + 1, j, k - 1)
```
